class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nitrogen-doping+Porous+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nitrogen-doping%20porous%20carbon">nitrogen-doping porous carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Antimony+Nanoparticles&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_antimony%20nanoparticles">antimony nanoparticles</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Antimony%2Fcarbon+Composite&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_antimony%2Fcarbon%20composite">antimony/carbon composite</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted increasing attention in recent years. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Research on negative electrode materials for SIBs has been focused on the following four aspects: (1) oxides and polyanionic compounds (such as phosphates) as topotactic insertion materials for sodium, (2) carbon materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> (3) mental selenides, sulfides, and oxides with a conversion reaction, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> and (4) p-block elements (phosphorus/phosphide, metals, and alloys) showing reversible sodiation–desodiation. In the above-mentioned categories, metals and alloys with distinctive electrical conductivities, high theoretical capacities, and ideal platforms represent a promising type of material for SIBs, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4, 5)</a> including Pb/Na<sub>15</sub>Pb<sub>4</sub> (484 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> Ge/NaGe (369 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> Bi/Na<sub>3</sub>Bi (385 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> Sn/Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> (847 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> Sn–Ge–Sb ternary alloy, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> Sb/Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> SnSb, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> and so on. However, the attempts to achieve a stable cyclic property is seriously hampered by drastic expansion and aggregation during the Na alloying–dealloying processes, e.g., 300% for Ge, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> 390% for Sb, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> and 525% for Sn. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14, 15)</a> Extensive efforts have been made to address the inherent structural stability problem of metals and their intermetallic electrodes. At present, these problems are usually eased by nanostructuring the active material, including the regulation of structure and morphology at the nanoscale, and the combination of complexes with phosphorus, graphite, and other related carbon structures. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 16 to 20">(16-20)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Among the most suitable anodes for Na-ion batteries, Na-alloy-based Sb, as a promising candidate, has drawn particular attention for the high theoretical capacity of 660 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and suitable potential (about 0.5–0.8 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) for sodiation–desodiation, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 21 to 25">(21-25)</a> while the inherently large volume changes and particle aggregation of Sb metal during sodiation usually lead to rapid capacity fading. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Thus, as is common with other metals, antimony and its combination with carbon, as well as their prospects and challenges, represent a thriving class of materials for Na-ion anode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 26 to 29">(26-29)</a> Recently, some composites have been presented with a Na storage capacity at about 500 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, such as Sb/C rods, Sb/C fibers, Sb/C microspheres, and so on, which have confirmed that the carbon material acts as a buffer and as a good conductive matrix during the cycling process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 30 to 36">(30-36)</a> In the midst of the above-mentioned materials, most carbon matrixes were synthesized by means of some complex methods, and the carbon content was higher, even over 50%, which may result in an expensive preparation cost and lower material capacity. Hence, it is still meaningful to develop carbon materials with more excellent electrochemical performances for the safe and economic preparation of Sb/C anode composites, especially with better cycling stability and higher Na storage capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">In all kinds of carbonaceous materials, hard carbon with low cost, moderate conductivity, abundance reserve, and corrosion resistance has been investigated theoretically and experimentally as an anode material for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref37 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 2 and 37 to 39">(2, 37-39)</a> One of the most talented commercial carbon candidates is porous carbon materials because of their large specific surface area and excellent chemical stability. In particular, hierarchical porous carbons, which can increase specific surface area, shorten ion diffusion distance, and provide spaces to relieve volume expansion, have attracted broad attention. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40, 41)</a> In addition, N-doping carbon matrixes may offer more defects and active sites without causing lattice mismatch, which is helpful for improving the electrochemical performances of the carbon material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42, 43)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">As shown above, porous carbon materials could be prepared in various ways, for example, utilizing different sizes of templates, heat treatment with molten salt, or carbonization from a polymer blend or polymer networks. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44, 45)</a> Especially activated carbons (ACs) are often required through chemical activation, and the commonly utilized activating agents include KOH, NaOH, H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, and ZnCl<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Meanwhile, polyaniline (PANI) has long been considered as a promising candidate for supercapacitor and battery electrodes because of its environmental stability and high electrical conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 47 to 49">(47-49)</a> In this work, a N-doping porous carbon (NPC) material was prepared via three-dimensional-structure PANI nanosheets using molten NaOH. Then, an Sb/NPC composite was successfully obtained by a low-cost reduction reaction. Most significantly, the Sb/NPC composites serving as anodes for SIBs demonstrate higher cycling stability than metallic Sb and show outstanding rate behavior. Therefore, it is worth noting that NPC synthesized through this method with PANI nanosheets as a precursor can be regarded as a potential carbon material for combination with a wide range of other metal materials.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Sample Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p">The synthesis of N-doping porous carbon (NPC) was as follows: 0.5 g of citric acid and 2.33 mL of phenylamine were dissolved in 125 mL of distilled water at 4 °C. Aqueous ammonium persulfate (10 mL, 1.9 g) was immediately transferred into the above mixture, and stirring continued for approximately 5 min. Next, the light-yellow solution was allowed to stand for 24 h, resulting in brown precipitate. The crude product was washed with deionized water several times until the filtrate became neutral and colorless. Then, the purified brown PANI powder was obtained through drying at 80 °C for 10 h. In the second stage, 0.5 g of as-prepared PANI was mixed with 5 g of NaOH, and the mixtures were heated to 600 °C for 2 h with a heat rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in Ar, followed by washing with HCl and distilled water several times. Finally, NPC was obtained via drying overnight in a vacuum oven at 80 °C.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The synthesis of Sb/NPC composites and Sb nanoparticles was as follows: 0.1 g of NPC and 2.9 g of C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>10</sub>O<sub>15</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub>K<sub>2</sub> powder were diffused in 100 mL of distilled water. Subsequently, an aqueous solution of NaBH<sub>4</sub> (1.5 g, 10 mL) was dropwise added to the above system at 60 °C with stirring for 1 h. Then, the Sb/NPC product was rinsed with distilled water five times and dried at 60 °C under vacuum for 10 h. Similarly, pure Sb nanoparticles were prepared in the absence of an NPC sample.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.2 Sample Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The specimens of samples were initially obtained by utilizing X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (Phillips X’pert Pro MPD diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation, λ = 1.5418 Å). Then, scanning electron microscopy (Hitachi JSM 7600, FE-SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (200 kV, JEOL JEM 2100F, TEM) were used to study the surface morphologies and structures of products. Also, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (ESCALAB 250 spectrometer, XPS) was further used to analyze the surface chemical composition. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller pore-size distributions, Barret–Joyer–Halenda (BJH) pore sizes, and surface areas of as-formed samples were measured using a surface-area and porosity analyzer by nitrogen physisorption at 77 K (BET, Micromeritics, ASAP 2020). Raman spectra were obtained with 532 nm excitation working at 15 mW on the Thermo Scientific DXR Raman instrument. Using a TGA 2950, thermogravimetric analysis of samples was conducted over a temperature range up to 800 °C under air atmosphere, and the heating rate was 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.3 Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Electrodes were obtained with a composition of carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder, active material, and super P with the weight ratios of 15:70:15 via pasting the homogeneous slurry of water onto copper foils. The loading density of material on the electrode foil is about 1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. In an Ar-filled glovebox, the CR2016 half-cells were assembled. The metallic sodium foils, porous polypropylene film, and NaClO<sub>4</sub> worked as counter electrodes, separator, and electrolyte, respectively. Ethylene carbonate and propylene carbonate mixed with a volume ratio of 1:1 in the electrolyte. At room temperature, cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves were performed with a Solartron Analytical device from 0.01 to 2.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. On an Arbin battery cycler (BT2000), the galvanostatic discharge–charge files were further performed. The Solartron Analytical device was used to collect electrochemical impedance (EIS) measurements at a voltage of 5 mV amplitude in the frequency ranging from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The schematic illustration for the formation of the Sb/NPC nanocomposite is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>. The PANI nanosheets were first obtained through polymerization at the stage of low temperature. Then, the porous structure of the NPC material could be prepared through a carbonizing process of PANI nanosheets within NaOH molten salt. At the temperature of 60 °C, the as-prepared NPC was uniformly dispersed in C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>10</sub>O<sub>15</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub>K<sub>2</sub> solution. It is noted that the abundant pore structure can provide more active sites to accommodate Sb nanoparticles. During the reduction, Sb nanoparticles were formed on the surface of NPC and were also well-embedded in the NPC material. Finally, the Sb/NPC composite was obtained with the size of Sb being about 30 nm.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/medium/am-2017-07964m_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0008.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Illustration for the Synthesis Process of NPC and the Sb/NPC Composite</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b07964&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphologic features of as-prepared PANI and NPC were observed by SEM and TEM. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows that the PANI sample presents a three-dimensional crisscrossed network structure composed of thin nanosheets. After carbonization, the NPC material, which exhibits a foamlike structure with abundant and open macropores, was achieved (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). The amount of pores with various pore sizes could be seen clearly in the NPC material through TEM, as exhibited in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/suppl_file/am7b07964_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. To better analyze the multipore structure of NPC, the nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherm was measured, as demonstrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a. The pore volume and specific surface area of NPC were calculated to be 0.78 cm<sup>–3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 437.7 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. On the basis of the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda pore-size distribution curve (inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a), we find that the pore distribution range is wide, which illustrates that various micro-, meso-, and macropores have all been formed in the NPC material. The as-prepared NPC material with hierarchical pores and large specific surface area might become one of the promising carbon materials for improving the capacity and stability of Sb materials as anodes for SIBs.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/medium/am-2017-07964m_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. SEM images of (a) PANI and (b) NPC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b07964&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/medium/am-2017-07964m_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms and the corresponding pore-size distributions of NPC (BET pore volume = 0.78 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, and surface area = 437.78 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>). (b) Raman spectra of NPC. (c) XPS survey spectrum and (d) high-resolution N 1s XPS spectra of NPC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b07964&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b displays the Raman spectra of NPC, where two peaks at ∼1357 and 1576 cm<sup>–1</sup> were indexed to the D band and the G band, respectively, confirming its amorphous structure. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value of the NPC is 0.89, indicating the existence of a high degree of disorder and large defects in the NPC structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Furthermore, the existence of N in NPC was demonstrated via XPS, displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c,d. The total doping nitrogen level of NPC is up to 11.15%. Three peaks centered at 400.8, 399.9, and 398.6 eV can be assigned to quaternary-N, pyrrolic-N and pyridinic-N. Among these doping nitrogen atoms, pyrrolic-N and pyridinic-N can be expected to enhance the sodium storage performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50, 51)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The XRD patterns of NPC, Sb, and Sb/NPC samples were investigated within the 2θ range 5–80° and are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a indicates that the NPC material is an undefined carbon material. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b,c, all of the XRD peaks are very consistent with those of the phases of hexagonal Sb (JCPDS 35-0732), suggesting that C<sub>8</sub>H<sub>4</sub>K<sub>2</sub>O<sub>12</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> was completely reduced to form metallic Sb. Moreover, the average sizes of Sb crystallites in pure Sb and the Sb/NPC composite were calculated to be 45 and 28 nm, respectively, utilizing the Scherrer equation for the (012) peaks of Sb. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> It can be seen that the size of Sb particles in the composite was smaller than that of the pure Sb particles because of the introduction of hierarchical porous NPC.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/medium/am-2017-07964m_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. XRD patterns of pure Sb nanoparticles, NPC, and Sb/NPC composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b07964&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The original morphology of the Sb/NPC composite was studied by SEM, TEM, and HRTEM, as seen in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. Upon comparison of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/suppl_file/am7b07964_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, the Sb nanoparticles with smaller size are dispersed homogeneously on the NPC material, which is very consistent with the XRD analysis. Furthermore, it can be seen that most of the small Sb nanoparticles with the average size of 30 nm are agglomerated and unevenly distributed within NPC in the sample of Sb/NPC composite from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c. It is worth stressing that some Sb nanoparticles are wrapped in the NPC material. Additionally, the elemental mappings of Sb/NPC (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e–h) illustrate a homogeneous distribution of Sb, N, and C atoms in the composite. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d indicates that the measured interlayer spacing of 0.325 nm is indexed to the (002) plane of Sb. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Also, the amorphous porous carbon structure for the NPC material could be further analyzed via the HRTEM image on the yellow area marked in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. For electrode materials, all of these are beneficial to relieve volume expansion and increase electrical conductivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/medium/am-2017-07964m_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) SEM of pure Sb nanoparticles. (b) SEM of Sb/NPC composite. (c) and (d) TEM and HRTEM images of Sb/NPC composite. (e) SEM image, and corresponding (f) C, (g) Sb, and (h) N elemental mapping images of Sb/NPC.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b07964&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The TGA was carried out to evaluate the Sb content of the Sb/NPC hybrid, as exhibited in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. The weight loss of the as-prepared Sb/NPC composite from room temperature to 250 °C might be due to the loss of chemisorbed water. Next, the weight began to decrease, which could be attributed to NPC oxidation. It is noted that the subsequent weight increase and loss of Sb/NPC are mainly attributed to the comprehensive effect of Sb and NPC oxidation with the products of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Hence, according to the reported equation, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> the content of Sb in this composite can be calculated to be about 84.7%.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/medium/am-2017-07964m_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) TGA profile of the Sb/NPC. (b) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm of the Sb/NPC composite and Sb nanoparticles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b07964&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The surface areas of the as-prepared Sb and Sb/NPC materials were investigated by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption measurements, as demonstrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. The specific surface areas (SSAs) of the pure Sb and Sb/NPC composite are calculated to be 19.1 and 92.76 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In addition, the Sb/NPC composite with 15.3 wt % NPC has a significantly higher surface area than Sb nanoparticles (about 20 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) on the basis of its composition, indicating that the presence of NPC could be effective to gain the small size of Sb nanoparticles again. Moreover, it is essential to retain the shortened ion-transport distance with the introduction of NPC material. N doping would also be beneficial to promote the electronic conductivity of carbonaceous materials and enhance surface capacitive effects. Above all, the NPC material with N doping and a porous structure could be considered one of the most promising carbon-based composite materials.</div><div class="NLM_p">The CV curves of the Sb/NPC electrode at a scanning rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> with a potential window from 0.01 to 2.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) are illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a. During the first cathodic scan, an obvious wide peak C1 at about 0.27 V can be ascribed to the conversion of crystalline Sb to the Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase, the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer, and an irreversible reduction from the amorphous carbonaceous framework of NPC. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The C1 peak split into the three peaks C2, C3, and C4 at about 0.29, 0.47, and 0.66 V in the subsequent cycles, respectively, which is a benefit from the fact that active Sb with the initial hexagonal phase has permanently transferred to be amorphous. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> Furthermore, only one strong peak, A1, is located at 0.92 V in the following first anodic process, which should be attributed to the desodiation reaction of formed Na<sub>3</sub>Sb to amorphous Sb. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> It is meaningful to note that the CV profiles, except those of the first cycle, are almost overlapped, showing the outstanding cycle stability of the Sb/NPC electrode during the discharge–charge tests. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b displays the discharge and charge voltage profiles of the Sb/NPC material between 0.01 and 2.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The charging and discharging plateaus of the Sb/NPC electrode in the initial three cycles are very consistent with the results of the CV curves. The first discharge capacity of the obtained Sb/NPC is 795.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, with a Coulombic efficiency of 65.9%. The irreversible specific capacity might be due to the decomposition of the electrolyte, the formation of SEI film in the first cycle, and the irreversible reactions between sodium ions and the oxygen-containing functional groups from NPC. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> In addition, the overlapping charging and discharging curves can confirm that the Sb/NPC electrode would display superior cycling stability.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/medium/am-2017-07964m_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical performance of the Sb/NPC microsphere electrode: (a) CV curves at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (b) charge–discharge profiles of the first, second, and third cycles at a fixed current of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b07964&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cycling performances of NPC, pure Sb, and Sb/NPC samples were further analyzed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a. The first discharge and charge capacities of the obtained NPC material are 446.1 and 216.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. This irreversible specific capacity mainly originates from the formation of the SEI film and the irreversible reactions between sodium ions and the oxygen-containing functional groups on the NPC electrode surface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> It is worth noting that the reversible charge capacity still remains 241.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles with a high Coulombic efficiency of 98.8%, illustrating that the NPC material displays excellent cycling stability. Additionally, the charge capacities of as-prepared pure Sb and Sb/NPC are 387.8 and 529.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles at the same current density, illustrating that the Sb/NPC composite exhibits enhanced cycling stability by the introduction of the NPC material. Compared with the initial reversible charge capacity, the cycling capacity retention of Sb/NPC is about 97.2% at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, the Sb/NPC electrode still shows outstanding cycling performance even at a high current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b. After 100 cycles, the charge capacity retention can still be up to 93.9% compared with the initial charge capacity, and the Coulombic efficiency has increased to 96.2% at the third cycle.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/medium/am-2017-07964m_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0007.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Comparative cycle performance of the Sb nanoparticle anode, NPC anode, and Sb/NPC composite anode at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> from 0.01 to 2.0 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. (b) Cycling performance for Sb/NPC cycled at currents of 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Rate performance of Sb and Sb/NPC at different rates from 100 to 3200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Nyquist plots of the Sb nanoparticle electrode and the Sb/NPC electrode after 3 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/asset/images/large/am-2017-07964m_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b07964&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, the rate capabilities of the Sb electrode and Sb/NPC electrode are evaluated at different current densities ranging from 100 to 3200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, as presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c. The average charge specific capacities for Sb/NPC composite are 561, 530, 503, 423, 357, and 287 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at various current densities of 100, 200, 400, 800, 1600, and 3200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. It is clear that the rate performance of the Sb/NPC composite is much higher than that of pure Sb nanoparticles. Considering recently reported Sb/C materials listed for comparison in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#tbl1" aria-label="Table 1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>, the Sb/NPC material displays superior cycling stability and rate performance. To reveal the reason for the improved electrochemical performances of the Sb/NPC composite, the electrodes before cycling and after 100 cycles were further investigated by SEM and elemental mapping (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/suppl_file/am7b07964_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). Integrated with the SEM image, the elemental mappings display that the Sb nanoparticles are uniformly dispersed in the hybrid carbon matrix consisting of NPC without high agglomeration formed during sodiation–desodiation, which is very consistent with the superior cycling stability.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Selected Performance Metrics of Sb/Carbon Composites between This Work and the Previous Reports</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap"><table class="table" border="0"><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">composite material</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">carnon content (%)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">L-rate capacity (mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>) (current density)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">H-rate capacity (mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>) (current density)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">cycling capacity retention (%), cycles (current density)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Sb@C coaxial nanotubes <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">39.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">460 (0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">370 (2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">50.6, 240 cycles (0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Sb/multilayer graphene <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">∼60</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">483 (0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">318 (2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">58.3, 200 cycles (0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">rodlike Sb/C composite <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">9.9</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">∼450 (0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">259 (2.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">∼95.7, 100 cycles (0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">pitayalike Sb@C spheres <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">59.2</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">655 (0.04&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">302 (3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">93.0, 100 cycles (0.04&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Sb@3D RCN film <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">32.0</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">630 (∼0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">∼200 (∼2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">64.1, 100 cycles (0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Sb/CCHI nanocomposite <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">59.1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">403 (0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">138 (3.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">92.3, 100 cycles (0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Sb–C framework film <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">33.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">∼442 (0.12&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">284 (∼1.4&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">∼100, 5000 cycles (∼3.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Sb@C yolk-shell spheres <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">17.6</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">600 (0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">329 (1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">∼70, 100 cycles (0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Sb/NPC (this&nbsp;work)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">15.3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">529.6 (0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">357 (1.6&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">97.2, 100 cycles (0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">15.3</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">529.6 (0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">357 (1.6&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">93.9, 100 cycles (0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p last">To get further insight into the difference of cyclability between Sb nanoparticles and Sb/NPC composite, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed. The results are presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/suppl_file/am7b07964_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. The EIS plots of two electrodes were measured before cycling and after 3 and 100 cycles. It can be seen that the contact and charge-transfer resistance of the Sb/NPC are much smaller than those of pure Sb from the high-frequency region, indicating that the conductivity of the composite is well-improved by NPC, and this property could contribute to the enhanced rate performances. In addition, the resistance values of the Sb/NPC electrode obviously decreased after cycling. These values also revealed the formation of the SEI film between the electrode and electrolyte as well as the presence of the activation process, both of which may lead to the outstanding cycling performance of Sb/NPC after the initial few cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> The resistance of the Sb electrode was increased after 100 cycles, which was caused by the drastic expansion and aggregation during the Na alloying–dealloying processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> In addition, the TEM and XPS above-mentioned results illustrate the existence of hierarchical pores and doping N atoms in the NPC material. Additionally, the chemical composition of Sb/NPC was also measured by XPS (Supporting Information, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/suppl_file/am7b07964_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). The results show that the peaks of Sb, C, and N were observed in the survey scan spectrum. The hierarchical pore structure can more easily allow electrolyte ion diffusion to the active material and also provides void space to accommodate the large volume expansion of Sb upon sodium intake. This specific structure can effectively improve the cycling stability and rate performance. More importantly, some Sb nanoparticles can be wrapped in NPC, and this structure could be effective to reduce the aggregation of Sb. Additionally, the NPC might protect some Sb nanoparticles against the formation of the SEI layer through direct contact with the electrolyte, which can effectively solve the issue of diminishing capacity.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">4 Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, NPC materials with hierarchical pores and N-doping atoms were prepared by the carbonizing process of three-dimensional PANI nanosheets within NaOH molten salt. Then, after chemical reduction, Sb nanoparticles with a smaller size (in comparison to the pure Sb sample) were successfully anchored in NPC, forming the Sb/NPC composite. It is excitingly found that Sb nanoparticles have been well-protected by and wrapped in hierarchical porous NPC to prevent the aggregation of Sb and buffer the volume expansion. Moreover, the existence of N atoms can be beneficial to promote the electronic conductivity of carbonaceous materials and enhance surface capacitive effects. Because of the introduction of NPC, the Sb/NPC electrode shows excellent cycling stability (cycling capacity retention of 97.2% at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles and 93.9% even at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles) and rate capacity (charge detailed capacity of 287 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 3.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>) as an anode material for SIBs. It is believed that the facile combination approach with the NPC material might be extended to other alloy materials for performance-enhanced SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b07964" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b07964</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">TEM and HRTEM of NPC, TEM image of Sb nanoparticles, SEM images and elemental mappings of Sb/NPC before cycling and after 100 cycles, Nyquist plots of the Sb and Sb/NPC electrode before cycling and after 100 cycles, and XPS spectra of Sb/NPC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/suppl_file/am7b07964_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b07964/suppl_file/am7b07964_si_001.pdf">am7b07964_si_001.pdf (1.2 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b07964" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Future outlook of sodium-ion batteries is discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo_Iht8id70u7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgyyiO3wv84Cg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFSiu7bL&amp;md5=d4497339c61083b2ec2c0568261e4909</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2015.09.091"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Balogun, M.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Qiu, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tong, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Review of Carbon Materials and Their Composites with Alloy Metals for Sodium Ion Battery Anodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Carbon</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">98</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">162</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">178</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A review of carbon materials and their composites with alloy metals for sodium ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Balogun, Muhammad-Sadeeq; Luo, Yang; Qiu, Weitao; Liu, Peng; Tong, Yexiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries are currently emerging as a replacement for lithium ion batteries due to sodium abundance in the earth's crust.  The recent development of sodium ion batteries anode has been achieved by the use of selected carbon as an anode.  The performance of the selected carbon materials such as the charge/discharge capacity, voltage profile and cyclic stability, depend strongly on the sizes, additives, electrolytes and structures.  Based on the contribution of the carbon-based materials as a replacement of the sodium ion batteries anode, much improvement has been achieved.  However, more investigations are still required in order to identify the suitable carbon materials that can provide and improve the anode properties.  In the same vein, alloy materials have gained increasing effort as high capacity anode and their combination with carbon have contributed to the development of sodium ion batteries.  In this review, we have accounted for the development of carbon-based materials and their composites with alloy materials as well as their prospects and challenges for sodium ion battery anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqLqTy97T_9sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgyyiO3wv84Cg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVOmsbvM&amp;md5=5bc4c118e6be905d663496bf2953e8d7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201700606"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chou, S.-L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, S.-X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Advances and Challenges in Metal Sulfides/Selenides for Next-Generation Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been proposed as candidates for replacing Li-ion batteries.  The authors examine the viability of Na-ion anode materials based on Na alloys or hard carbons in terms of volumetric energy d.  Due to the bigger size of the Na atom compared to the Li atom, Na alloys would lead to anode materials with about half the volumetric energy d. of their Li analogs.  Volumetric energy densities obtainable with sodiated hard carbons would also be significantly less than those obtainable with lithiated graphite.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The vol. expansion behavior and volumetric energy densities of metal alloy neg. electrodes (AxM) for metal-ion batteries based on A = Li, Na, K, Mg, Ca and Al are compared.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Here we provide the first report on several compns. of ternary Sn-Ge-Sb thin film alloys for application as sodium ion battery (aka NIB, NaB or SIB) anodes, employing Sn50Ge50, Sb50Ge50, and pure Sn, Ge, Sb as baselines.  Sn33Ge33Sb33, Sn50Ge25Sb25, Sn60Ge20Sb20, and Sn50Ge50 all demonstrate promising electrochem. behavior, with Sn50Ge25Sb25 being the best overall.  This alloy has an initial reversible specific capacity of 833 mAhg-1 (at 85 mAg-1) and 662 mAhg-1 after 50 charge-discharge cycles.  Sn50Ge25Sb25 also shows excellent rate capability, displaying a stable capacity of 381 mAhg-1 at a c.d. of 8500 mAg-1 (∼10C).  A survey of published literature indicates that 833 mAhg-1 is among the highest reversible capacities reported for a Sn-based NIB anode, while 381 mAhg-1 represents the optimum fast charge value.  HRTEM shows that Sn50Ge25Sb25 is a composite of 10-15 nm Sn and Sn-alloyed Ge nanocrystallites that are densely dispersed within an amorphous matrix.  Comparing the microstructures of alloys where the capacity significantly exceeds the rule of mixts. prediction to those where it does not leads us to hypothesize that this new phenomenon originates from the Ge(Sn) that is able to sodiate beyond the 1:1 Na:Ge ratio reported for the pure element.  Combined TOF-SIMS, EELS TEM, and FIB anal. demonstrates substantial Na segregation within the film near the current collector interface that is present as early as the second discharge, followed by cycling-induced delamination from the current collector.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpVb3oOuj1ecbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhv4gBCHvElYg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXkvVyqs78%253D&amp;md5=3b95d3d0fa5e586a5a5c9ce5fb4ec0ce</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1149/2.080403jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ellis, L.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ XRD study of silicon, lead and bismuth negative electrodes in nonaqueous sodium cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ellis, L. D.; Wilkes, B. N.; Hatchard, T. D.; Obrovac, M. N.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">161</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. alloying of sodium with silicon, lead and bismuth was studied by in-situ X-ray diffraction.  No evidence was found for sodium insertion into silicon at temps. up to 60°C.  Lead was found to catalytically decomp. electrolyte, hindering sodiation.  This could be avoided by applying a high current pulse to the cell to sodiate the lead surface.  Once the surface was sodiated, further sodiation of lead could proceed at low currents.  The sodiation of lead followed a path that differs from the equil. phase diagram and from that described in earlier reports.  During sodiation, Na9Pb4 was formed with a previously unreported structure that was found to be isostructural with Na9Sn4.  It was found that Na could be reversibly inserted into bismuth.  The mechanism for bismuth sodiation follows equil. phase behavior.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo0vCh28j3qI7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljlOqjA5hmcrg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXkt1Wqtrk%253D&amp;md5=6ed3a505ee165bd021448dca8d6c552b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2012.10.014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Datta, M.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tin and graphite based nanocomposites: Potential anode for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Datta, Moni Kanchan; Epur, Rigved; Saha, Partha; Kadakia, Karan; Park, Sung Kyoo; Kumta, Prashant N.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">225</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Pure Sn and a homogeneous nanocomposite of Sn and graphite (C), denoted as Sn/C, have been studied as a suitable anode for Na ion batteries.  The Sn/C nanocomposites have been synthesized by high energy mech. milling (HEMM) of pure Sn and graphite of nominal compn. C-70% Sn.  Pure microcryst. Sn (≤44 μm) exhibits a 1st discharge capacity ∼856 mA-h g-1 which is close to the expected theor. capacity, however, it shows a large 1st cycle irreversible loss (∼67%) and the anticipated inevitable rapid fade in capacity expectedly due to structural failure of the electrode.  However, the resultant Sn/C based nanocomposite, synthesized by HEMM after 1h of milling, exhibits a 1st cycle discharge capacity ∼584 mA-h g-1 with a 1st cycle irreversible loss ∼30%.  The Sn/C nanocomposite shows a 1st cycle charge capacity of ∼410 mA-h g-1 with improved capacity retention in comparison to pure Sn displaying 0.7% fade in capacity per cycle up to 20 cycles when cycled at a rate of approx. C/8.  SEM anal. indicates that the structural integrity and microstructural stability of the Sn/C nanocomposite during the alloying/dealloying processes appear to be the primary factors contributing to the good cyclability obsd. in the above HEMM derived nanocomposite suggesting its promise as a potential anode for Na-ion systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrQLHelWVsLyrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljlOqjA5hmcrg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhslKqur%252FF&amp;md5=d2501e6449630ca6e7ff6eee4e0aeed7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.08.095"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nam, D.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical synthesis of a three-dimensional porous Sb/Cu2Sb anode for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nam, Do-Hwan; Hong, Kyung-Sik; Lim, Sung-Jin; Kwon, Hyuk-Sang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A 3-dimensional porous Sb/Cu2Sb electrode is prepd. by electrodeposition on a Cu foam substrate, and its electrochem. properties are studied by a galvanostatic test for Na-ion batteries.  The Sb/Cu2Sb electrode exhibits outstanding cycle stability and excellent rate capability; the charge capacity is sustained at 485.64 mA h g-1 over 120 cycles with a high coulombic efficiency of 97%, and the charge capacity retention is ∼70% of the capacity at 0.1 C-rate, even at high 3 C-rate.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Monodisperse SnSb nanocrystals for Li-ion and Na-ion battery anodes: synergy and dissonance between Sn and Sb</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Meng; Walter, Marc; Kravchyk, Kostiantyn V.; Erni, Rolf; Widmer, Roland; Kovalenko, Maksym V.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">455-459</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report a facile chem. synthesis of monodisperse colloidal SnSb nanocrystals (NCs) via reaction between Sn NCs and SbCl3 in oleylamine under reducing conditions.  In comparison with individual Sn and Sb NCs and their mixts., we show that through the creation of SnSb alloyed NCs the Li-ion storage properties are enhanced due to combination of high cycling stability of Sb with higher specific Li-ion storage capacity of Sn.  In particular, stable capacities of above 700 and 600 mA h g-1 were obtained after 100 cycles of charging/discharging at 0.5C and 4C rates, resp. (1C corresponding to a c.d. of 660 mA g-1).  Furthermore, Na-ion storage capacities of &gt;350 mA h g-1 and &gt;200 mA h g-1 were obtained at 1C and 20C rates, resp.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Antimony (Sb) is an attractive anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with a high theor. capacity of 660 mAh g-1.  However, its practical application is greatly hindered by the rapid capacity fading which is largely due to the large vol. expansion during sodiation.  Tuning the morphol. and structure at the nanoscale or using carbonaceous materials as the buffer layer is essential to address this issue.  Here, a facile carbon-coating coupled with a thermal-redn. strategy has been developed to synthesize unique Sb@C coaxial nanotubes.  With different annealing time, the hollow space and the amt. of Sb inside the tube can be easily tuned by the partial evapn. of Sb.  The as-obtained Sb@C nanotubes exhibit excellent sodium storage properties.  The remarkable electrochem. performance results from the unique coaxial nanoarchitecture.  Specifically, it delivers a high specific capacity of 407 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1 after 240 cycles.  Furthermore, a stable capacity of 240 mAh g-1 can be retained at 1.0 A g-1 even after 2000 cycles.  Most importantly, high capacities of 350 mAh g-1 and 310 mAh g-1 can be achieved at large current densities of 10 and 20 A g-1, resp., which represents the best rate performance among the reported Sb-based anode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpmon6HTiU9LrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljlOqjA5hmcrg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XpsVOqsrs%253D&amp;md5=19ea4b459ee66a164418399db8b12d47</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201402943"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tao, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ultrasmall Sn Nanoparticles Embedded in Carbon as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A new type of hierarchical tin@carbon composite composed of graphene carbonaceous matrix and well-confined tin nanoparticles with a typical size of ∼15 nm (denoted as F-G/Sn@C) is developed and investigated as anode material for Li-ion and Na-ion storage.  The two dimensional graphene backboned matrix not only acts as a confinement layer preventing the tin nanoparticles from aggregating during the material prepn., but also functions as a phys. barrier to buffer the vol. change effect during charge/discharge processes.  As a consequence, the composite demonstrates excellent rate performance and cycling stability for both Li-ion and Na-ion storage.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exfoliated MoS2 Sheets and Reduced Graphene Oxide-An Excellent and Fast Anode for Sodium-ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sahu, Tuhin Subhra; Mitra, Sagar</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Scientific Reports</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Three dimensional (3D) MoS2 nanoflowers are successfully synthesized by hydrothermal method.  Further, a composite of as prepd. MoS2 nanoflowers and rGO is constructed by simple ultrasonic exfoliation technique.  The crystallog. and morphol. studies have been carried out by XRD, FE-SEM, TEM, HR-TEM and EDS etc.  Here, XRD study revealed, a composite of exfoliated MoS2 with expanded spacing of (002) crystal plane and rGO can be prepd. by simple 40 min of ultrasonic treatment. Mater. Interfaces</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">8</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">7147</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">7155</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Building Self-Healing Alloy Architecture for Stable Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes: A Case Study of Tin Anode Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mao, Jianfeng; Fan, Xiulin; Luo, Chao; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7147-7155</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The rational design of anode materials is a challenge in developing sodium ion batteries.  Alloy anodes provide high gravimetric and volumetric capacities but suffer the short cycle life as a result of the continuous and accumulated pulverization, resulting from a large vol. change during the cycling process.  Herein, using pure Sn, an irreversible conversion reaction combined with an alloy reaction (SnO), and a reversible conversion reaction combined with an alloy reaction (Sn4P3) as samples, the pulverization and aggregation of the alloy anode can be partially recovered and the accumulation of pulverization and aggregation during charge/discharge cycles can be terminated using a reversible conversion reaction combined with an alloy reaction.  The cycling stability of three Sn-based anodes increases in order of Sn4P3 &gt; SnO &gt; Sn.  The enhancement in Sn4P3 can be attributed to a reversible reaction of Sn4P3 + 9Na ↔ 4Sn + 3Na3P, which repairs the cracks, damage, and aggregation of Sn particles that occurred in the alloy process of 4Sn + 15Na ↔ Na15Sn4 during cycling and, hence, terminates the pulverization.  The repair mechanism looks like the self-healing feature, where the damage can be healed by itself.  Therefore, the suggested mechanism can be called self-healing, while the repaired anode can be termed as the self-healing anode.  The use of self-healing strategies to build an electrode architecture is new and highly desirable because it can increase the cycle life and provide a general approach toward stable electrode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpWw5beIJBM1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liMstkyVvUozQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xjs1WjsL0%253D&amp;md5=b0bdee575b24a49017d3e87579d7c7a7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2013.05.025"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Baggetto, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Keum, J. The reaction is accompanied with a reversible capacity close to 350 mAh g-1, which matches the value expected for the formation of NaGe.  The electrode capacity retention is stable over 15 cycles but subsequently declines.  However, using fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) electrolyte additive pos. improves capacity retention and promotes the formation of a thinner SEI.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoconfined antimony in sulfur and nitrogen co-doped three-dimensionally (3D) interconnected macroporous carbon for high-performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Chenglong; Li, Weihan; Yang, Zhenzhong; Gu, Lin; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

The Sb@S,N-3DPC electrode shows a high reversible capacity of 482 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, displaying good capacity stability (∼93% of the initial discharge capacity after 500 cycles at 100 mA g-1).                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Uniform yolk-shell iron sulfide-carbon nanospheres for superior sodium-iron sulfide batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yun-Xiao; Yang, Jianping; Chou, Shu-Lei; Liu, Hua Kun; Zhang, Wei-xian; Zhao, Dongyuan; Dou, Shi Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
